<div id="review_882601205" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/882601205" />
    <a title="Sherry Elmer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20805054-sherry-elmer"><img alt="Sherry Elmer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369803487p2/20805054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/882601205?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherry Elmer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherry Elmer" href="/user/show/20805054-sherry-elmer">Sherry Elmer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of mythology; fans of C.S. Lewis</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=thinking-authors">thinking-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=worth-reading-again">worth-reading-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=c-s-lewis">c-s-lewis</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer882601205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11292284506443233574">This is the third time for me to read this book, and I only started it again because the Ambleside online moms were reading it. Although I loved the book, I did not feel a reread was important at this time. But, since my lovely friends were reading, why not?<br/>On this third reading I was stopped after just the first paragraph: “I am old now and have not much to fear from the gods. I have no husband nor child, nor hardly a friend, through whom they can hurt me.”<br/>This hurt to read. It was only the day</span>
  <span id="freeText11292284506443233574" style="display:none">This is the third time for me to read this book, and I only started it again because the Ambleside online moms were reading it. Although I loved the book, I did not feel a reread was important at this time. But, since my lovely friends were reading, why not?<br />On this third reading I was stopped after just the first paragraph: “I am old now and have not much to fear from the gods. I have no husband nor child, nor hardly a friend, through whom they can hurt me.”<br />This hurt to read. It was only the day before that I had thought almost the same thing, although not so eloquently. I had the thought that it is time for a new dream, and immediately I had the follow-up thought, “Having a dream is a fast way to be sure it won’t come true.” The thought shamed me, because the thought was a (wrong) judgement of God. He has proved Himself so many times, and yet… Do I still sometimes find myself thinking, as Lewis said in another book, “We are not necessarily doubting that God will do the best for us; we are wondering how painful the best will turn out to be.” I could see that I was. <br />And that is what I find this book excelling at—inciting me to examine myself, to check my motives, to probe the inner recesses of my heart. I can think of no other novel that affects me more powerfully in this way. <br />Which means that although I love this book, reading it isn’t necessarily a pleasant experience. But it is a good and needful one, and for that I am thankful. I am thankful, too, for the readers at Ambleside online for inspiring me to read this again at such a time as this. <br /><br />February 21, 2020<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />I once heard someone ask, What if you spent your whole life climbing to the top of the proverbial ladder only to discover that the ladder had been leaning against the wrong building all along. I can imagine how devastating this would be. Buy how much worse to discover at the end of your life that the person you thought you were was an illusion, and you are actually the last thing you ever wanted to be. <br /><br />This is one of the questions C.S. Lewis raises in this profound book. Till We Have Faces begins as a retelling of the ancient Cupid and Psyche story, but by the end, it grabs you by the throat and forces you to look into the mirror, much the way Orual's father once did to her. Maybe that look will not be too painful. Or, maybe, like Orual, you will say, “'I am Ungit.' Without question it was true. It was I who was Ungit. That ruinous face was mine. I was that Batta-thing, that all-devouring womblike, yet barren, thing. Glome was a web—I was the swollen spider, squat at its center, gorged with men's stolen lives.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="11292284506443233574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15938161203842186037">So, two cavaets:<br/><br/>1) I didn't know the original myth for most of this book so I may have missed a bit of the inside references and stuff (if there are any?). But, knowing the myth now, I think Lewis did a BEAUTIFUL job retelling it.<br/><br/>2) The ending kinda confused me, maybe because I was reading it really fast before I had to go to church. Definitely a novel/ending that deserves to be read slowly so that one can catch all the nuances.<br/><br/>With all that being said...<br/><br/>THIS IS AN INCREDIBLE BOOK. It allllmost </span>
  <span id="freeText15938161203842186037" style="display:none">So, two cavaets:<br /><br />1) I didn't know the original myth for most of this book so I may have missed a bit of the inside references and stuff (if there are any?). But, knowing the myth now, I think Lewis did a BEAUTIFUL job retelling it.<br /><br />2) The ending kinda confused me, maybe because I was reading it really fast before I had to go to church. Definitely a novel/ending that deserves to be read slowly so that one can catch all the nuances.<br /><br />With all that being said...<br /><br />THIS IS AN INCREDIBLE BOOK. It allllmost hit five stars and probably would have, if not for the issues I mentioned above. (Well, they aren't even issues. Just...stuff. :P) The story, the characters, the message, the WORLD BUILDING - everything was perfect. *adds C.S. Lewis to the smallish list of male authors who write from a female POV exceedingly well*<br /><br />C.S. Lewis, y'all.<br /><br />Just...the wonder of him.</span>

<div id="review_1686996610" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1686996610" />
    <a title="Hana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10571559-hana"><img alt="Hana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458172215p2/10571559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1686996610?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hana" href="/user/show/10571559-hana">Hana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi">fantasy-sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1686996610" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3281335533068698961">Jealousy, insecurity and abuse are the toxic and combustible fuels that fire this story of a tribal royal family. Great storytelling with memorable characters who even in their failures engage the reader. I kept turning the pages compulsively until the final fifty pages [more later].<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17343.Till_We_Have_Faces' title='Till We Have Faces by C.S. Lewis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Till We Have Faces</a> also intersected rather neatly and serendipitously with my current non-fiction read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1831667.The_Horse__the_Wheel_and_Language_How_Bronze_Age_Riders_from_the_Eurasian_Steppes_Shaped_the_Modern_World' title='The Horse, the Wheel and Language How Bronze-Age Riders from the Eurasian Steppes Shaped the Modern World by David W. Anthony' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Horse, the Wheel and Language: How Bronze-Age Riders from the Eurasian Steppes Shaped the Modern World</a>. My 19</span>
  <span id="freeText3281335533068698961" style="display:none">Jealousy, insecurity and abuse are the toxic and combustible fuels that fire this story of a tribal royal family. Great storytelling with memorable characters who even in their failures engage the reader. I kept turning the pages compulsively until the final fifty pages [more later].<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17343.Till_We_Have_Faces" title="Till We Have Faces by C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">Till We Have Faces</a> also intersected rather neatly and serendipitously with my current non-fiction read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1831667.The_Horse__the_Wheel_and_Language_How_Bronze_Age_Riders_from_the_Eurasian_Steppes_Shaped_the_Modern_World" title="The Horse, the Wheel and Language How Bronze-Age Riders from the Eurasian Steppes Shaped the Modern World by David W. Anthony" rel="nofollow noopener">The Horse, the Wheel and Language: How Bronze-Age Riders from the Eurasian Steppes Shaped the Modern World</a>. My 1956 edition of Lewis' novel has vaguely Egyptian and Mesopotamian illustrations but Lewis' fantasy kingdom of Glome has more in common with the Bronze Age cultures around the Black Sea that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/836966.David_W__Anthony" title="David W. Anthony" rel="nofollow noopener">David W. Anthony</a> studies. <br /><br />Glome is a world under constant threat and now facing the inexorable reality of change. Marginal farm-based economies; constantly shifting tribal rivalries; threats from other cultures (mostly the Greeks as embodied by a marvelous character, The Fox, tutor and counselor extraordinaire); an old female-centered religion decaying at the head but still moving the populace; an equally old male-centered military culture looking towards blood-letting as the route to wealth and domination. Glome is also a land in which human sacrifice, the sacrifice not of willing warriors but of the innocent, is ennobled. Onto this scene come three royal sisters of Glome: one so ugly that her only hope seems to be to veil herself, one born so beautiful that those who see her declare her a goddess, and one whose desires and devices might bring her sisters and the kingdom to an end.<br /><br />Alas, the last fifty pages....<br /><br />You know how it is when you are almost on the edge of waking up? And you are in the middle of dream that's mostly boring and doesn't make much sense? And part of your brain is saying this is a dumb dream! Yup! Part II is just like that. <br /><br />Part II is the sort of thing PC editors of the author's time insisted that the writer add. It's the sort of thing writer feel they have to add because...they want to prove they are smart, with it, whatever. My recommendation wake yourself up before Part II, forget all the commentary about Psyche and Cupid, etc., and just enjoy the book as pure fantasy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3281335533068698961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125161738" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125161738?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=based-on-myth-saga-etc">based-on-myth-saga-etc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=greek-roman">greek-roman</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125161738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17630191876143409989">Till We Have Faces is a retelling of the myth of Cupid and Psyche. I read it as such, and not as a theological text, though there's elements of that there if that's how you choose to approach it. I chose to approach it as a story, though, as a myth retelling -- and I suspect it's perfectly possible to do both at once.<br/><br/>It's a beautifully told story, and one that feels real, psychologically and in terms of feeling like a real place, with real people. The basic details of the original story are that</span>
  <span id="freeText17630191876143409989" style="display:none">Till We Have Faces is a retelling of the myth of Cupid and Psyche. I read it as such, and not as a theological text, though there's elements of that there if that's how you choose to approach it. I chose to approach it as a story, though, as a myth retelling -- and I suspect it's perfectly possible to do both at once.<br /><br />It's a beautifully told story, and one that feels real, psychologically and in terms of feeling like a real place, with real people. The basic details of the original story are that Psyche is very beautiful, and Aphrodite is jealous, and has her people sacrifice her. Cupid falls in love with her, though, and saves her, but she is never allowed to see his face and know who he is. When her sisters find her, they are jealous and persuade her to light a lamp when she is with him. She does so, and he is so beautiful that she can't look away, and carelessly spills some oil on his skin. He wakes and is angry, and leaves her to wander the world and face Aphrodite's wrath. Eventually, they are reunited and she becomes a goddess.<br /><br />Lewis' retelling is questioning what it would have been like if the sister had told Psyche to light a lamp not out of jealousy, but out of love and concern for her. His Orual loves very jealously, true, and is not blameless by any means, but she does what she does out of concern for Psyche and love for her.<br /><br />He adds a lot to the myth. The women are less foolish, for example, and even central: Orual becomes queen in her own right. And there's obviously a lot more by way of interpersonal relationships: Psyche and Orual, Orual and Bardia, Orual and the Fox.<br /><br />It made my heart ache quite a lot. Like I said, it's beautiful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17630191876143409989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21858824?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/886733-heather">Heather</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886733-heather?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21858824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6934637557761433447">One of my favorite books of all times. It changed me.<br/><br/>I don't know exactly what to say about it that would be informative. It is Mr. Lewis' spin on the tale of Psyche and Cupid. However, it is so much more. It touches topics like paganism, jealousy, love, bias, etc.<br/><br/>But, more than anything, the part of this book that sticks with me continually is Orual's plea to the gods. The deep philosophical points of this scene (and the storyline of her journey to this point) are so profound. <br/><br/>This work, like C</span>
  <span id="freeText6934637557761433447" style="display:none">One of my favorite books of all times. It changed me.<br /><br />I don't know exactly what to say about it that would be informative. It is Mr. Lewis' spin on the tale of Psyche and Cupid. However, it is so much more. It touches topics like paganism, jealousy, love, bias, etc.<br /><br />But, more than anything, the part of this book that sticks with me continually is Orual's plea to the gods. The deep philosophical points of this scene (and the storyline of her journey to this point) are so profound. <br /><br />This work, like C.S. Lewis' other works, is not a light-hearted read. It is deep, philosophical, and slow-going. But SO worth it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6934637557761433447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3140408168" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3140408168" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21391485-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458919645p2/21391485.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3140408168?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/21391485-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3140408168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14760957137983794590">It's hard for me to explain what this book is even about. On its surface, it's about a king's ugly daughter, Orual, who is fiercely possessive and protective of her younger half-sister, Psyche, who is even more beautiful than Orual is ugly. They live in an ancient kingdom in the Middle East-ish sort of area, I guess? And their father is a tyrant, they have a sister who's a fool, and they have a Greek slave called The Fox for a tutor. <br/><br/>The Fox teaches them all sorts of fairly athiestic philosophy.</span>
  <span id="freeText14760957137983794590" style="display:none">It's hard for me to explain what this book is even about. On its surface, it's about a king's ugly daughter, Orual, who is fiercely possessive and protective of her younger half-sister, Psyche, who is even more beautiful than Orual is ugly. They live in an ancient kingdom in the Middle East-ish sort of area, I guess? And their father is a tyrant, they have a sister who's a fool, and they have a Greek slave called The Fox for a tutor. <br /><br />The Fox teaches them all sorts of fairly athiestic philosophy. Their own culture worships a rock-goddess and believes in many deities. But Orual basically worships her sister. Until she loses her. And gets freaked out by what she finds when she goes looking for her sister.<br /><br />Basically, the whole thing is all about what it's like to be an unbeliever who loves a person who becomes a believer. How left out you would feel, then how rejected, how angry, how confused. How betrayed and bewildered. Orual cannot accept or believe what happened to Psyche, and her unbelief drives her to desperation. It's masterfully told, of course, and I think that when I re-read it, I'll enjoy it even more because it wasn't until the very end that I finally got what Lewis was saying about unbelief and belief, so I spent most of the book going, "Um, wait, what?"</span>
  <a data-text-id="14760957137983794590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('O5mpxBnsLfUcPDo4j5UJrGF8eFR88NqKh9MNQB3sRQagTJV+spXRoUtIsiFMbyxG/Vl1GDhmQXiEL0wt39G2+g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ySN1R8lluiUpYr4IcByopV/F8KoK69BMxLMOYikf6N5S9kn9YhxGcX4WNhGz5o1Pw+D95k59S77HT08P6yIbIg==')})
  }

Not every Lewis reader is a Lewis scholar. Most pick up one of his stories or essays out of curiosity, not with academic intent nor with the hope of reading all of his works.  After teaching this novel for a decade, I determined to write a guide that would help any reader fully appreciate C.S. Lewis’s final, and favorite, work of fiction.  In Till We Have Faces, Lewis paints an unexpected picture of redemption. As Orual relates her selective memoir, she finds that her questions about the gods fall away. While Lewis’s retelling brings an unusual completion to the original Greek myth of Cupid and Psyche, this companion is a blend of summary and commentary that brings greater understanding of the characters, symbolism, and implied Christian themes outside of a classroom discussion. Lewis said that upon reading this astonishing 19th-century fairy tale he &quot;had crossed a great frontier,&quot; and numerous others both before and since have felt similarly.\n\nIn MacDonald\'s fairy tales, both those for children and (like this one) those for adults, the &quot;fairy land&quot; clearly represents the spiritual world, or our own world revealed in all of its depth and meaning. At times almost forthrightly allegorical, at other times richly dreamlike (and indeed having a close connection to the symbolic world of dreams), this story of a young man who finds himself on a long journey through a land of fantasy is more truly the story of the spiritual quest that is at the core of his life\'s work, a quest that must end with the ultimate surrender of the self.\n\nThe glory of MacDonald\'s work is that this surrender is both hard won (or lost!) and yet rippling with joy when at last experienced. As the narrator says of a heavenly woman in this tale, &quot;She knew something too good to be told.&quot; One senses the same of the author himself.\n\nNewly designed and typeset in a modern 6-by-9-inch format by Waking Lion Press.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12037994490027868734\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div Written in the author\'s early forties in the last years of the fourth century A.D. and during his first years as a bishop, they reflect on his life and on the activity of remembering and interpreting a life. Books I-IV are concerned with infancy and learning to talk, schooldays, sexual desire and adolescent rebellion, intense friendships and intellectual exploration. Augustine evolves and analyses his past with all the resources of the reading which shaped his mind: Virgil and Cicero, Neoplatonism and the Bible. This volume, which aims to be usable by students who are new to Augustine, alerts readers to the verbal echoes and allusions of Augustine\'s brilliant and varied Latin, and explains his theological and philosophical questioning of what God is and what it is to be human. Lewis and the Art of Writing is written for readers interested in C. S. Lewis, the writing life, and in becoming better writers. Lewis stands as one of the most prolific and influential writers in modern history. His life in letters offers writers invaluable encouragement and instruction in the writing craft. In Lewis, writers don\'t just learn how to write, they also learn something about how to live. This volume explores Lewis\'s life in, as well as his practice of, writing. From his avid reading life, to his adolescent dreams to be a great poet, through his creative failures, to his brilliant successes, to his constant encouragement of other writers, C. S. Lewis and the Art of Writing celebrates one of the twentieth-century\'s greatest authors. &quot;&quot;Corey Latta has accomplished a rare feat, penning an engaging and exquisite treatment of C. S. Lewis as a voracious reader and writer\'s writer. It will be relished and savored by Lewis aficionados, and take readers of every sort on a fascinating guided tour of Lewis\'s literary adventures with an assortment of disparate scenic stops along the way. A book worthy of the subject, it\'s a fitting tribute to Lewis, often haunting in its beauty and perspicacity, on occasion downright stirring. It shows the indissoluble link between Lewis\'s prescient and prodigious writing and his wide reading, features a treasure trove of eminently practical advice for the aspiring writer, and fills readers with a poignant sense of the nobility of the writing vocation.&quot;&quot; --David Baggett, author (with Jerry Walls) of Good God: The Theistic Foundations of Morality (2011) and God and Cosmos: Moral Truth and Human Meaning (2016). &quot;&quot;Most interpreters attribute C. S. Lewis\'s literary success to his content, his brilliant insights, and his vivid imagery. But Corey Latta offers a fresh approach, inviting readers to examine Lewis\'s style--what Lewis learned from his own reading of great authors, what he observed about his own writing process, and what advice he offered to apprentice writers. Latta shows that Lewis\'s eminence derives as much from his manner of writing as from his matter, how he learned to embody the classical definition of rhetoric, \'a good man speaking well.\'&quot;&quot; --David C. Downing, R. W. Schlosser Professor of English, Elizabethtown College; Author of several award-winning books on C. S. Lewis, including Planets in Peril and The Most Reluctant Convert &quot;&quot;C. S. With his own lively style and passionate commitment to truth and beauty, Latta serves as both navigator for readers on this educational journey and model of its result.&quot;&quot; --Marybeth Baggett, Associate Professor of English, Liberty University; former Associate Editor of Christ and Pop Culture; Associate Editor, MoralApologetics.com &quot;&quot;In this engaging and enlightening volume, Corey Latta shows how C. S. Lewis\'s reading, writing, and faith formed the man. He’s stumbled along the way, made mistake after mistake, and yet has continually encountered the grace of God through an encouraging family, a Christ-centered community of artists in the church, and the power of truth, beauty, and goodness in Scripture and the arts.   While there are many books about writing, none deal first-hand with the intersection of songwriting, storytelling, and vocation, along with nuts-and-bolts exploration of the great mystery of creativity. In Adorning the Dark, Andrew describes six principles for the writing life:  \nserving the work\nserving the audience\nselectivity\ndiscernment\ndiscipline\nand community\n  Through stories from his own journey, Andrew shows how these principles are not merely helpful for writers and artists, but for anyone interested in imitating way the Creator interacts with his creation.   This book is both a             var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis">About C.S. Lewis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1564671804p3/1069006.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4779022941908808005"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br/><br/><br/>Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge</span>
  <span id="freeText4779022941908808005" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br /><br />Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than thirty books, allowing him to reach a vast audience, and his works continue to attract thousands of new readers every year. His most distinguished and popular accomplishments include <i>Mere Christianity</i>, <i>Out of the Silent Planet</i>, <i>The Great Divorce</i>, <i>The Screwtape Letters</i>, and the universally acknowledged classics <i>The Chronicles of Narnia</i>. To date, the Narnia books have sold over 100 million copies and been transformed into three major motion pictures.<br /><br />Lewis was married to poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/467360.Joy_Davidman" title="Joy Davidman" rel="nofollow noopener">Joy Davidman</a>.<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1988716._W_H__Lewis" title=" W.H. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener"> W.H. Lewis</a> was his elder brother]</span>
  <a data-text-id="4779022941908808005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
